</a><br><br>If you like to mix a little hick lit in to your reading repertoire, this title might be of interest to you, but if you are a lover of the character redemption arc or happily-ever-after, you best just keep on keeping on. On the other hand, if you’re like Mitchell and myself, sometimes pitch black is your favorite color . . . and Donald Ray Pollock writes that color oh so very well.<br><br><i>The Devil All the Time</i> is a story with an ensemble cast that revolves a bit around one central figure, Arvin Russell. The book follows Arvin from his days as a youngster in post WWII Ohio through his young adulthood in the 1960s. The supporting cast run the gamut from God warriors to serial killers to child molesters. (Told ya it was bleak.) The best way I can sum up the story is if this . . . <br><br><a href="http://www.freeimagehosting.net/commercial-photography/texas/houston/" rel="nofollow noopener">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Shelby *trains flying monkeys* by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/18711982-kristin">Kristin</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1053760-shelby-trains-flying-monkeys?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer649756551" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer98962039090290409">Not a book with fairy tales and happy endings.<br/><br/>This book shows the darkness that can linger in people's souls. <br/><a href='http://imgur.com/iCSncGw' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1404579892ra/10260343.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/>Willard Russell believes if he and his son Arvin pray over his "prayer log" long enough it can save his doomed wife Charlotte from the cancer ravaging her body. It might now be enough to just pray alone though..so he adds some sacrificial blood.<br/><br/>So begins this tale. Setting in rural Ohio and West Virginia. Pollock shows the side of poor rural life that I hope to never see.<br/><br/>The characte</span>
  <span id="freeText98962039090290409" style="display:none">Not a book with fairy tales and happy endings.<br><br>This book shows the darkness that can linger in people's souls. <br><a href="http://imgur.com/iCSncGw" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1404579892ra/10260343.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Willard Russell believes if he and his son Arvin pray over his "prayer log" long enough it can save his doomed wife Charlotte from the cancer ravaging her body. It might now be enough to just pray alone though..so he adds some sacrificial blood.<br><br>So begins this tale. Setting in rural Ohio and West Virginia. Pollock shows the side of poor rural life that I hope to never see.<br><br>The characters in this book do come to life. In order to send chills down your spine and sweep you into their lives. The author writes beautifully and I do hope to see more from him. I love this type of dark disturbing story. <br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/XaKGYTS" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1404579892ra/10260344.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><i>The man nodded and stared out the window. "It's hard to live a good life," he said. "It seems like the Devil don't ever let up."</i><br><br>After reading this I looked at the author's bio. He worked 30 years in a papermill in a rural area. That old adage write what you know? I still have chills up my back.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_170557077" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/170557077" />
    <a title="Lou" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3699303-lou"><img alt="Lou" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1312212794p2/3699303.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/170557077?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lou" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lou" href="/user/show/3699303-lou">Lou</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer170557077" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6457790371614749499">Read the <a href='http://more2read.com/review/donald-ray-pollock-recommends-books/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Donald Ray Pollock Recommends Books page,</a> put together from an interview i had with Donald Ray Pollock<a href='http://more2read.com/review/interview-with-donald-ray-pollock/' rel='nofollow noopener'> Here</a>.<br/><br/>Beware some brutal characters contained within this story.<br/>The name Pollock strikes up images in my head of the paintings of Jackson Pollock the painter, that splatter art. Well this Pollock is just as creative with his storytelling that leaves a deep branding in your mind and soul of a human stain of evil characters, that will stay with you well after you have finished this book. </span>
  <span id="freeText6457790371614749499" style="display:none">Read the <a href="http://more2read.com/review/donald-ray-pollock-recommends-books/" rel="nofollow noopener">Donald Ray Pollock Recommends Books page,</a> put together from an interview i had with Donald Ray Pollock<a href="http://more2read.com/review/interview-with-donald-ray-pollock/" rel="nofollow noopener"> Here</a>.<br /><br />Beware some brutal characters contained within this story.<br />The name Pollock strikes up images in my head of the paintings of Jackson Pollock the painter, that splatter art. Well this Pollock is just as creative with his storytelling that leaves a deep branding in your mind and soul of a human stain of evil characters, that will stay with you well after you have finished this book. I am thinking of similarities here to Authors of likes of Jim Thompson who wrote ‘The Killer inside me’ and Daniel Woodrell.<br /><br /><br />In the town Knockemstiff Pollock has crammed a motley crew of all the grittiest and evil characters you could find. These characters are ones you don’t want to tell your kids that they exist on this planet. You will find in these pages slices of the worst of the worst. The show is on and the location is Knockemstiff, you will get more than you fair slice of the darkest of souls that you could muster up. The hero for me in this story is Arvin he dishes out some sweet justice that makes you really rejoice in accolades of well done i been waiting for that evil good for nothing to taste some justice. The author is hitting topics here head on, ones that the Vatican need to take heed of and act, too many darn wolves in Sheep’s clothing.<br /><br /><br />Some really swell writing from Pollock. Believe the hype that is out there right now about this writer and his works.<br /><br /> <b>
  <blockquote>“Unless he had whiskey running through his veins, Willard came to the clearing every morning and evening to talk to God. Arvin didn’t know which was worse, the drinking or the praying. As far back as he could remember, it seemed that his father had fought the Devil all the time.”<br /><br /> “it sounded more like they were practicing some sort of voodoo instead of anything Christian.”</blockquote>
</b><br /><br />There is a video interview at the Ohio channel with this author of a show called <a href="http://www.ohiochannel.org/MediaLibrary/Media.aspx?fileId=131943" rel="nofollow noopener">Writers talk</a>.<br />.<br /><br /><br /><a href="http://www.more2read.com/?review=the-devil-all-the-time-by-donald-ray-pollock" rel="nofollow noopener">www.more2read.com/?review=the-devil-all</a></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3218831562?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susanne  Strong" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susanne  Strong" href="/user/show/4514828-susanne-strong">Susanne  Strong</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3218831562" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10151029525162520587">4 Stars. <br/><br/><b>Depravity, Desperation, Evil Incarnate. “The Devil All the Time.”</b> <br/><br/><i>“It’s hard to live a good life. It seems like the devil don’t ever let up.”</i><br/><br/>Raised by his mother and father in Knockemstiff, Ohio, Arvin grew up going to a prayer log everyday with his father, bringing “gifts” to the log as blood sacrifices, in hopes that they would be enough to heal his mother Charlotte of cancer. Poor Arvin.<br/><br/>There is a traveling pastor whose “faith” has cured him of his fear of spiders. Such that he prove</span>
  <span id="freeText10151029525162520587" style="display:none">4 Stars. <br /><br /><b>Depravity, Desperation, Evil Incarnate. “The Devil All the Time.”</b> <br /><br /><i>“It’s hard to live a good life. It seems like the devil don’t ever let up.”</i><br /><br />Raised by his mother and father in Knockemstiff, Ohio, Arvin grew up going to a prayer log everyday with his father, bringing “gifts” to the log as blood sacrifices, in hopes that they would be enough to heal his mother Charlotte of cancer. Poor Arvin.<br /><br />There is a traveling pastor whose “faith” has cured him of his fear of spiders. Such that he proves it to an entire congregation. His faith makes him believe that he can cure anyone of, and from anything. <br /><br />Husband and wife Carl and Sandy are the freakiest of all. Traveling serial killers. Yep, that is what I said. A couple that kills together, stays together. You should see their collection of photographs! <br /><br /><b>The crazy and completely sick stories in “The Devil All the Time” by Donald Ray Pollack will intertwine in the most incredible of ways. They will in fact make your head spin. Though this novel is well, not your average novel, it is utterly compelling, entertaining and one helluva thrilling read. This is a brilliant addition to the genre of Southern Literature and I am so glad that I read it (prayer log, spiders and all). </b><br /><br />Thank you to Donald Ray Pollack for the wholly depraved and fascinating read.<br /><br />Published on Goodreads on 4.10.20.</span>

<div id="review_183202161" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/183202161" />
    <a title="Trudi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1416912-trudi"><img alt="Trudi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1446613110p2/1416912.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/183202161?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trudi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trudi" href="/user/show/1416912-trudi">Trudi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=all-in-the-family">all-in-the-family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=modern-lit">modern-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=crime-mystery">crime-mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=my-desert-island">my-desert-island</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=a-sense-of-place">a-sense-of-place</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=heart-of-darkness">heart-of-darkness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=prose-that-sings">prose-that-sings</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=on-my-kobo">on-my-kobo</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer183202161" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4525324536237430066">Jesus wept, but this is the real goods people -- gritty, raw, uncompromising prose that snaps and bites at your soft spots. I find it curious that so many people have shelved Pollock's sophomore novel as horror, because while it is horrifying in places, and deals with some chilling characters, horror it is not. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/263436987' rel='nofollow noopener'>In his review</a> of Pollock's debut <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1704719.Knockemstiff' title='Knockemstiff by Donald Ray Pollock' rel='nofollow noopener'>Knockemstiff</a>, Kemper uses the terms <i>redneck noir</i> and <i>hick lit</i> and that's much closer to capturing what this novel is offering to anyone who dares pick it </span>
  <span id="freeText4525324536237430066" style="display:none">Jesus wept, but this is the real goods people -- gritty, raw, uncompromising prose that snaps and bites at your soft spots. I find it curious that so many people have shelved Pollock's sophomore novel as horror, because while it is horrifying in places, and deals with some chilling characters, horror it is not. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/263436987" rel="nofollow noopener">In his review</a> of Pollock's debut <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1704719.Knockemstiff" title="Knockemstiff by Donald Ray Pollock" rel="nofollow noopener">Knockemstiff</a>, Kemper uses the terms <i>redneck noir</i> and <i>hick lit</i> and that's much closer to capturing what this novel is offering to anyone who dares pick it up. <br /><br />One of the things that impressed me so much here is how well Pollock is able to juggle multiple narrative threads, do each of them justice, and have them collide and intersect with one another in a convincing, <i>satisfying</i> way. He makes it look so easy. <i>Of course</i> it all comes together in the end, but I can't help but think how easily this could have been majorly flubbed, or how forced and <i>deus ex machina</i> it could have read in the hands of a lesser writer. <br /><br />This is a dark novel, full of dark deeds, it almost suffocates you. This is not a novel of redemption or hope but an unflinching look into the dark heart of man (and woman), bringing all the monsters that lurk there out into the light, into the open to be seen and feared. I found parts of this novel very difficult to read, and not because Pollock is explicit in his descriptions, because he isn’t. He refrains from showing the reader everything, leaving room for what you can <i>imagine</i> -- and isn’t that always worse? I know it is for me. He gives you just enough rope to hang yourself with. But his prose is vivid nevertheless, and there are scenes from this novel that I will <i>never</i> forget. <br /><br />Unlike Frank Bill's short story collection <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10713935.Crimes_in_Southern_Indiana_Stories" title="Crimes in Southern Indiana Stories by Frank Bill" rel="nofollow noopener">Crimes in Southern Indiana </a>, Pollock injects an emotionality here and manages to humanize his characters even as he shows how monstrous they can be. While <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/205196008" rel="nofollow noopener">I absolutely loved <i>Crimes</i></a>, there is a humanity distinctly missing from Bill's characters -- the violence and hatred taking precedence over everything else. Pollock's writing here is closer to Woodrell's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/112525.Winter_s_Bone" title="Winter's Bone by Daniel Woodrell" rel="nofollow noopener">Winter's Bone</a>, another outstanding piece of writing in this vein of small town, hardscrabble folk. All men represent the very best of their craft however, when it comes to capturing a sense of place and the people who live there. <blockquote>Unless he had whiskey running through his veins, Willard came to the clearing every morning and evening to talk to God. Arvin didn't know which was worse, the drinking or the praying. As far back as he could remember, it seemed that his father had fought the Devil all the time. ~<i>The Devil All The Time</i></blockquote> <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4525324536237430066" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ɗẳɳ  2.☊" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ɗẳɳ  2.☊" href="/user/show/12058788-2">Ɗẳɳ  2.☊</a>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12058788-2?shelf=goldilocks-zone">goldilocks-zone</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12058788-2?shelf=grit-lit">grit-lit</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1096302171" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17641049835028842505">Friends, when I look out into those teaming masses, that sea of nameless faces mindlessly distracted by some mobile device, do you know what it is that I see reflected back in those blank stares? I tell ya what I see is <b>a whole mess of dull-witted, primped, and pampered sissies, as soft as a doughnut</b>. I reckon it’s past time to put down that lousy screen, crack open this good book, and take a glimpse of how things once was in our not-so-distant past. Then you ought to thank your lucky stars you’</span>
  <span id="freeText17641049835028842505" style="display:none">Friends, when I look out into those teaming masses, that sea of nameless faces mindlessly distracted by some mobile device, do you know what it is that I see reflected back in those blank stares? I tell ya what I see is <b>a whole mess of dull-witted, primped, and pampered sissies, as soft as a doughnut</b>. I reckon it’s past time to put down that lousy screen, crack open this good book, and take a glimpse of how things once was in our not-so-distant past. Then you ought to thank your lucky stars you’re all tucked in safe and secure in the comfort of today’s luxuries. <br /><br />Friends, this was a time of hard men and simple living. A time when a man was still that apex predator and a woman was just another pawn in that savage man’s game. There was a place, in the backwoods and side roads of rural America, where that man played by his own set of rules. When everything you got in this life you had to fight, scrape and claw just to hold on to. And life itself was as fleeting as a shooting star that crossed the evening sky, ready to blink out at any moment. <br /><br />Better get right with the Lord because one false step and that’s all she wrote, son. But, “it’s hard to live a good life, [when] <b>the Devil don’t ever let up</b>.” <br /><br />Keep to mind though these stories ain’t for the faint of heart. If you’re looking for a happy ending, you best take that nonsense elsewhere. This was some rough and tumble living. Ain’t no guarantees on tomorrow, so you better snatch up all you can get today. Then get on outta there while the gettin’s good. And if by some miracle you do make it past all them pitfalls to the end of that long and winding road, don’t you think it prudent friends to take just one moment, and drop down on your knees and thank the good Lord above for protecting and keeping you from <b>The Devil All the Time?</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="17641049835028842505" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_241398836" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/241398836" />
    <a title="Paquita Maria Sanchez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2508169-paquita-maria-sanchez"><img alt="Paquita Maria Sanchez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594814608p2/2508169.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/241398836?book_show_action=true">Nov 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paquita Maria Sanchez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paquita Maria Sanchez" href="/user/show/2508169-paquita-maria-sanchez">Paquita Maria Sanchez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2508169-paquita-maria-sanchez?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer241398836" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6779750693769099356">Gritty crime thrillers* are not generally my cup of tea, be they in fancy pants, shiny new hardcovers in the Literature section or shitty paperbacks in the mass-market rows of shame where all the bored housewives hang out. There seems to more often than not be this sort of straight to the point, unsalted cracker style to the writing which leaves me feeling dissatisfied. I mean, sure, I know some people in real life who parrot the same words and stories, use "like" and "ummm" as ways to pause in </span>
  <span id="freeText6779750693769099356" style="display:none">Gritty crime thrillers* are not generally my cup of tea, be they in fancy pants, shiny new hardcovers in the Literature section or shitty paperbacks in the mass-market rows of shame where all the bored housewives hang out. There seems to more often than not be this sort of straight to the point, unsalted cracker style to the writing which leaves me feeling dissatisfied. I mean, sure, I know some people in real life who parrot the same words and stories, use "like" and "ummm" as ways to pause in a sentence all the time, and tell a story using "and then he said, and then she said, and then he was like, and then I was all like," etc. Then again, I'm not generally expecting to find myself seduced by words in real life considering that <i>I work in a bar</i>; a bar where I hear, and let's see if you get it, Toby Keith's "I Love This Bar" being played on the jukebox by a bar patron at least one time during every single shift. (Because we're in a bar! That you love! So you sing along! Every night! Ahhhhh, f*ck!) Wait, where was I? Right, genre fiction.<br /><br />As I was saying, there's this tendency in crime thrillers for purdy prose (and intricately constructed characters, for that matter) to be sacrificed in the service of increasing the plot's momentum, and if I'm being totally honest, I am a flowery, Wilde-loving bitch with her head (and wardrobe, and book/record collections) wedged firmly in the past. What can I say, I love me a good monologue. Despite all that, I'm trying to be better about reading things actually written during my lifetime, and this is one such attempt. So this is basically genre fiction, except it's actually really flippin' good. My gripe above does hold some weight here: there aren't any what you would call "flabbergasting" or "groundbreaking" revelations in this thing, and no immortal quotables to be stripped from the text. However, what there <i>is</i> is a whole lot of stacked, horrifying, and carefully placed imagery concerning savagery and the violent tendencies within all humans, rich to poor, spoiled to abandoned, male or female. The world holds many a charming tune for the snake within us all. Whether our motivations be sadistic, masochistic, righteous, vengeful, righteously vengeful, or just plain hormone-driven, we all carry this reptilian streak which, with the proper circumstantial nurturing, can lead to an explosion of unspeakable violence. All this is sorta old news, but Pollock handles it in a way that is engaging in more than just some torture porn way. He stirs up some feelings, and I mean more than just fear. Unlike all that horror movie stuff, this all feels...Real. Likely. Highly probable. Probably has happened pretty much this way somewhere at some time. <br /><br />I particularly enjoyed the approach that Pollock took concerning religion, addressing its uses as both a salve and a weapon, or more specifically as a justification for and refutation of violence and exploitation. These parts hit my Flannery bone, which is always a welcome injury. The first section of the book, which includes a truly grotesque sacrificial prayer altar depicted in graphic detail (think maggots, bones clanking in the wind, layers of blood on rotting, patchily-haired flesh driven through with nails and other types of general ick) was my favorite of the many eerie little stories in this interlocking collection of horrific events. The imagery throughout the novel, though described somewhat dryly, still manages to resonate. If this paragraph hasn't scared you away, then by all means GO! READ! IS GOOD! <br /><br />*Excluding movies, of course. Some of my all time favorite movies are gritty crime thrillers. Speaking of which, this would (will one day) make a great movie. My advice? Give it to Fincher. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6779750693769099356" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1071926230" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1071926230" />
    <a title="Richard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4677640-richard"><img alt="Richard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472613597p2/4677640.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1071926230?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard" href="/user/show/4677640-richard">Richard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=2015-reads">2015-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=small-town-secrets">small-town-secrets</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=cuuntry-grit-lit">cuuntry-grit-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1071926230" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14759630371319687944"><b>*4.5 Stars*</b><br/>Sometimes I find it difficult to put into words what I really liked about a particular book. This novel is one of those. It's a nihilistic portrait of rural life in Ohio and West Virginia for a disparate group of fucked up individuals as they struggle to not only survive within the parameters that life and fate has stuck them with, but also with their own inherent impulses and desires. It's an admirable, well-conceived debut novel that is brutal but manages to not feel gratuitous. The</span>
  <span id="freeText14759630371319687944" style="display:none"><b>*4.5 Stars*</b><br />Sometimes I find it difficult to put into words what I really liked about a particular book. This novel is one of those. It's a nihilistic portrait of rural life in Ohio and West Virginia for a disparate group of fucked up individuals as they struggle to not only survive within the parameters that life and fate has stuck them with, but also with their own inherent impulses and desires. It's an admirable, well-conceived debut novel that is brutal but manages to not feel gratuitous. The novel's strength lies in it's prose, it's ideas, the attention to detail, it's imagery (the "church" in the woods...whoa) and highly intricate characters that are both appalling and captivating. You can't help but get swept up in their miserable lives to see where they go next. All of this combines to create a heavy mood and atmosphere that Pollock evokes with his simple but precise prose. It's an atmosphere that's distinctive and one that I haven't experienced in many other books. It's like the novel takes place in it's own savage dimension...</span>
  <a data-text-id="14759630371319687944" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3494059662" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3494059662" />
    <a title="Maxwell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1381728-maxwell"><img alt="Maxwell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539318702p2/1381728.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3494059662?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maxwell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maxwell" href="/user/show/1381728-maxwell">Maxwell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/54520140-the-devil-all-the-time">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3494059662" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3061465563748853784">[<b>2.5 stars</b>]<br/><br/><b>You can read my <a href='https://letterboxd.com/maxwellwinton/film/the-devil-all-the-time/<br />' rel='nofollow noopener'>review of the film adaptation here</a>.</b><br/><br/>After seeing the trailer for the <a href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=EIzazUv2gtI' rel='nofollow noopener'>upcoming Netflix adaptation</a> of this novel, I was intrigued. I still am after reading this as I think it will work well as a film. The criss-crossing storylines, gritty atmosphere and seedy characters will likely translate to screen better than this novel worked for me.<br/><br/>My biggest issue with this novel is the pacing. I usually don't complain about books being too short, but this one moved so quickly thro</span>
  <span id="freeText3061465563748853784" style="display:none">[<b>2.5 stars</b>]<br /><br /><b>You can read my <a href="https://letterboxd.com/maxwellwinton/film/the-devil-all-the-time/&lt;br /&gt;" rel="nofollow noopener">review of the film adaptation here</a>.</b><br /><br />After seeing the trailer for the <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=EIzazUv2gtI" rel="nofollow noopener">upcoming Netflix adaptation</a> of this novel, I was intrigued. I still am after reading this as I think it will work well as a film. The criss-crossing storylines, gritty atmosphere and seedy characters will likely translate to screen better than this novel worked for me.<br /><br />My biggest issue with this novel is the pacing. I usually don't complain about books being too short, but this one moved so quickly through the plot that you barely got to get to know the characters. Consequently, the characters felt flat. Like pawns moving in the author's game toward a final conclusion. Granted, it was an interesting story, extremely horrific at times (seriously, be warned it's quite gruesome), but the ways in which the story unfolds felt a bit predictable. <br /><br />The characters were fascinating but not likeable. I'm fine with reading about unlikeable characters if they at least serve as good anti-heroes. Arvin, arguably our main character, is the most compelling at times, born into and lives through unfortunate circumstances. He is torn between dark and light, following the path of the Devil or the Lord. Sandy and Carl are prowlers who take advantage of people at the whims of their own pleasure. And then there are the terrible religious figures in this novel, a couple of very questionable reverends. <br /><br />Because the story manages to follow so many main characters, and quite a few side characters, we never dive deeply into the psyche of any one of them. Paired with pretty straightforward writing, this made the story move briskly but lack depth.<br /><br />There's nothing really bad about this book. It's perfectly fine but nothing special to me. I was expecting something more psychologically compelling and gripping, à la Dennis Lehane. Instead I was served a pretty standard Southern gothic/horror-esque novel that I'd probably just rather watch on screen.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3061465563748853784" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2413731397" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2856182478117444671">4.5 stars<br/><br/>It feels like I have been stuck with quite a few tepid books recently, that is until I picked up this book.<br/><br/>Sheeze!!! Did Quinton Tarantino’s evil twin write this?<br/><br/>It hits you over the head and drags you to very dark places. The book was physically difficult to put down as my fingers cramped from gripping it so hard.<br/><br/>This is not for the sensitive reader, if you struggle with dark books, with hyper violence and disgusting characters in some god forsaken back-woods and then please don’t read</span>
  <span id="freeText2856182478117444671" style="display:none">4.5 stars<br /><br />It feels like I have been stuck with quite a few tepid books recently, that is until I picked up this book.<br /><br />Sheeze!!! Did Quinton Tarantino’s evil twin write this?<br /><br />It hits you over the head and drags you to very dark places. The book was physically difficult to put down as my fingers cramped from gripping it so hard.<br /><br />This is not for the sensitive reader, if you struggle with dark books, with hyper violence and disgusting characters in some god forsaken back-woods and then please don’t read this book. For the rest of you – go read it now!<br /><br />These characters are all connected and each one is drawn in such detail that they stand out like a movie playing in your head.<br /><br />From a father who will do anything to save his dying wife, even blood sacrifices, to a husband wife team cruising the countryside for hitchhikers to mutilate and kill, corrupt cops, even more corrupt priests and everything in between. <br /><br />The thing is, if you look past all the ugly there are also some very poignant moments, the writing is superb and it’s a book I will not soon forget. <br /><br />Highly recommended</span>
  <a data-text-id="2856182478117444671" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1691693371" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1691693371" />
    <a title="Margitte" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7481492-margitte"><img alt="Margitte" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361772481p2/7481492.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1691693371?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Margitte" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Margitte" href="/user/show/7481492-margitte">Margitte</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=american-novel">american-novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=2016-read">2016-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=family-sagas">family-sagas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=american-author">american-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=community">community</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1691693371" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1015804440338670196"><br/>From the blurb:<br/><blockquote><b>From the acclaimed author of <i>Knockemstiff</i>—called “powerful, remarkable, exceptional” by the <i>Los Angeles Times</i>—comes a dark and riveting vision of America that delivers literary excitement in the highest degree. </b><br/><br/>In <i>The Devil All the Time</i>, Donald Ray Pollock has written a novel that marries the twisted intensity of Oliver Stone’s <i>Natural Born Killers</i> with the religious and Gothic over­tones of Flannery O’Connor at her most haunting. <br/><br/>Set in rural southern Ohio and West Virginia, <i>The </i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText1015804440338670196" style="display:none"><br />From the blurb:<br /><blockquote><b>From the acclaimed author of <i>Knockemstiff</i>—called “powerful, remarkable, exceptional” by the <i>Los Angeles Times</i>—comes a dark and riveting vision of America that delivers literary excitement in the highest degree. </b><br /><br />In <i>The Devil All the Time</i>, Donald Ray Pollock has written a novel that marries the twisted intensity of Oliver Stone’s <i>Natural Born Killers</i> with the religious and Gothic over­tones of Flannery O’Connor at her most haunting. <br /><br />Set in rural southern Ohio and West Virginia, <i>The Devil All the Time</i> follows a cast of compelling and bizarre characters from the end of World War II to the 1960s ...</blockquote>A few other books in this hillbilly-noir - genre comes to mind. Authors such as Cormack McCarthy, Peter Englund, John Steinbeck, Chris Offut, et al.<br /><br />I could not finish any of Cormack McCarthy's books, but with <i>The Devil All the Time</i> I simply could not put the book down, although I often could not decide if I wanted to puke from, or praise the actions of the characters. My conclusion was that it was brilliantly written. What a journey!<br /><br />There were no angels on this road. I suspect that even God might have had a few doubts from time to time. The author managed to humanize evil to such an extent that I wanted to jump up and down with joy for the anti-hero's actions, even urging him on! Now is THAT bizarre or what?<br /><br />The original religious seven deadliest sins (hubristic pride, greed, lust, malicious envy, gluttony, inordinate anger, and sloth) have been depicted in all forms of art. In <i>The Devil All the Time</i> the carnal collection takes glorious center stage in the now ghost town of Knockemstiff, southern Ohio.<br /><br />The three main stories:<br /><b>One:</b>-- Private Willard Russell comes home to Coal Creek, West Virginia from WWII on a Greyhound bus, and stops over in a little paper mill town Meade, Ohio for refreshments at the Wooden Spoon. He falls in love with Charlotte, a beautiful waitress. He did not mind that the meat loaf was dry, the green beans were mushy, or the rolls as hard as a lump of #5 coal. With Charlotte serving the meal, it was the best one he ever had! <br /><br />Arriving home, his mother wants him to marry Helen, but Willard returns to the Wooden Spoon to find Charlotte and marry her. They have a perfectly normal son, Arvin Eugene. Willard did not want his family to live among the perverted degenerates in town. The country was going to hell in a hurry as it is. He developed a urgent need to pray for his family in case something bad might happen to his wife and son. <br /><br />He has not prayed for many years, neither could he connect with God in a cramped apartment or even in church. He finds the house on top of the Mitchell Flats which he rents for thirty dollars a month from the first class conniving prick of a lawyer called Henry Delano Dunlap, a plump girlish man with shiny immaculate fingernails and liver-colored lips. Willard will prove that poor people do not always want what the rich people have. <br /><br />He had one rehearsed prayer he repeats every month after his visit to the lawyer's office: <i> "Thank you, God, for giving me the strength to keep my hands off Henry Dunlap's fat fucking neck. And let the sonofabitch have everything he wants in this life, though I got to confess, Lord, I sure wouldn't mind seeing him choke on it someday."</i> <br /><br /> In the woods near their home, Willard set up a prayer log where he conducts sacrificial rituals and teaches Arvin to pray ...pray ... pray, goddamn it! Charlotte had cancer and God was not listening. <i>"Make Him hear you, you understand?". Arvin prayed until his throat was raw and blistered. But though Arvin kept screaming for the Lord's mercy, he didn't feel anything and none came. Some of the people in Kockemstiff closed their windows, even with the heat. Others kept a light on the rest of the night, offered prayers on their own. </i><br /><br />A few days later Willard told Arvin: <i>" I am thinking we might take us a little trip. Go down and visit your grandma in Coal Creek. Maybe stay for a while. You can meet uncle Earskill, and that girl they got living with them would be just a little younger than you. You'll like it there."</i><br /><br />Thus begins Arvin's journey on the road where angels fear to tread and God makes a u-turn or two Himself ... <br /><br /><b>Two:</b>-- Willard's parents, Tom and Emma Russel, become the proteges of orphaned Helen Hatton, when her family is killed in a house fire. Emma has promised God that her son Willard will marry Helen if God brings him back alive from the war. There was one glitch though. Helen had a long, horsey face, wore wire-rim glasses set crooked on her long face, had a square chin and wore dirty bonnets. An odor of old sweat swirled around her which watered a passerby's eyes. Helen looked just like her grandmother Rachel, considered by many the homeliest woman who ever walked the ridges of Greenrier County. <br /><br />Emma knew, when Willard married Charlotte instead, that her promise was broken, and that God will turn the tables on her and her family, like He did with her husband Tom . Emma was a God-fearing woman, and Tom was a moonshiner with a little bit of bad luck in his past... <br /><br />Emma expected accidents or diseases hitting her family as a result of her broken promise. Edith Maxwell, over on Spud Run, had three pin headed blind children. However, when Helen announces her marriage, Emma was satisfied that God has forgiven her for her brief loss of trust. <br /><br />Helen marries a preacher, Brother Roy Laferty, who has a disabled sidekick, Brother Theodore Daniels. The two cousins, the fat boy in the wheelchair, Theo, and Roy, the tall skinny fellow, perform sermons with spiders and sad guitar music coming from Theodore's banged-up guitar in their effort to save some lost souls. It is the only way they could attract listeners to their traveling church. Helen was not scared of the spiders at all. <i>"Oh, don't worry," the girl said, as she started down off the porch, "they ain't too bad once you get used to them."</i> ...<br /><br />When one of the spiders bites Roy and leaves him for a couple of months stayed up in his closet with a swollen head, puffed up big as a pumpkin, he decides to join a traveling circus. But that was only after Helen disappears, leaving her perfectly normal daughter, Lenora, behind with Tom and Emma Russel. Roy and Theo mysteriously disappears as well. God is not always present when humans try to perform miracles ... and Lenora, poor thing, looked just like her mother ...<br /><br /><b>Three</b>-- Deputy Sheriff Leo Bodecker of Knockemstiff, a despicable, corrupt policeman, has a sister, Sandy. She marries a good-for-nothing photographer, Carl Henderson. Sandy also works at the Wooden Spoon, dishing out her sexual favors as a dessert to eager customers. Complaints is received and Deputy Bedecker has to act to save his re-election campaign. The Henderson couple also enjoys an annual road trip on various routes to find models for Carl's passion in photography ...<br /><br />I deliberately do not share the plot or give any clues to what happens to these people. That is for the reader to enjoy! I wanted to introduce the people behind their actions. Ordinary people with good intentions, until circumstances interfere. Who are we to cast the first stones, after all, right?<br /><br />These three stories enfold from the end of World War II to the 1960s. A bizarre chain of events, almost Gothic in nature, brings this tale to an even more bizarre ending, so outrageous, that even the reader is happily pouring encores on the anti-hero!<br /><br />It was a huge relief to close the book! I felt immensely happy to escape! I wanted to embrace my world and yell "Oh how do I LOVE thee, let me count the ways!" <br /><br />The narrative is filled with music, melody, magic. Drama, and tension keep the plot moving forward. Everything about this book is picturesque. Intense. Unbearably rich in scope, characters, background, meaning. The prose romanced my soul until the very end. <br /><br />There is a distinction between this author's writing style and all the others mentioned above. He brings warmth and compassion to his characters. The story also reminds me a lot of <i>Tobacco Road</i> by Erskine Caldwell. In fact, I would rather compare it with this classic gem than any other authors's works. The story has that same depth of human spirit to it. It is certainly one of the highlights in my reading life and hopefully it will become one of the American greatest reads of all times.<br /><br />ABSOLUTELY RECOMMENDED.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1015804440338670196" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('KlMpzLvFX0sKWe9ioaw46YxMgJvZvLHEQBwreHGMrNU+3q7uEOoe+aPu5IsH2MjDzc5vyGzccQhw8Cz+v4XsOA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

Published when s<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText5875786120056020772\" style=\"display:none\">The debut novel from New York Times–bestselling author Meg Wolitzer, a story of three college students’ shared fascination with poetry and death, and how one of them must face difficult truths in order to leave her obsession behind.  Published when she was only twenty-three and written while she was a student at Brown, Sleepwalking marks the beginning of Meg Wolitzer’s acclaimed career. Filled with her usual wisdom, compassion and insight, Sleepwalking tells the story of the three notorious “death girls,” so called on the Swarthmore campus because they dress in black and are each absorbed in the work and suicide of a different poet: Sylvia Plath, Anne Sexton, and Wolitzer’s creation Lucy Asher, a gifted writer who drowned herself at twenty-four. At night the death girls gather in a candlelit room to read their heroines’ work aloud.  But an affair with Julian, an upperclassman, pushes sensitive , struggling Claire Danziger—she of the Lucy Asher obsession-–to consider to what degree her “death girl” identity is really who she is. And locals aren’t thrilled that their small town has been made infamous thanks to Maggie’s father. As a kid, Dandro would temper the everyday tension with flights of fancy, finding refuge in toys and animals and insects rather than in the unpredictable adults around him. He perceptively details the effects of poverty and addiction on a family while maintaining a child’s innocence for as long as he can.\n\nKing of King Court spans from Travis’s early childhood through his teen years, focusing not only on the obviously abusive actions but also on the daily slights and snubs that further strain relations between him and his parents. Alongside his birth father committing crimes and shooting up, King of King Court lingers on scenes of him criticizing Travis and his siblings. Dandro gives equal heft to these anecdotes, emphasizing how damaging even relatively slight traumas can be to a child’s worldview.\n\nAs Travis matures into young adulthood and begins to understand the forces shaping his father’s toxic behaviors, the story becomes even more nuanced. Travis is empathetic to his father’s own tragic history but unable to escape the cycle of misconduct and reprisals.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/784866.Donald_Ray_Pollock">About Donald Ray Pollock</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/784866.Donald_Ray_Pollock"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1240540889p3/784866.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/784866.Donald_Ray_Pollock">Donald Ray Pollock
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,406
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12641226759240952259">Donald Ray Pollock was born in 1954 and grew up in southern Ohio, in a holler named Knockemstiff. He dropped out of high school at seventeen to work in a meatpacking plant, and then spent thirty-two years employed in a paper mill in Chillicothe, Ohio. He graduated from the MFA program at Ohio State University in 2009, and still lives in Chillicothe with his wife, Patsy. His first book, Knockemstif</span>
  <span id="freeText12641226759240952259" style="display:none">Donald Ray Pollock was born in 1954 and grew up in southern Ohio, in a holler named Knockemstiff. He dropped out of high school at seventeen to work in a meatpacking plant, and then spent thirty-two years employed in a paper mill in Chillicothe, Ohio. He graduated from the MFA program at Ohio State University in 2009, and still lives in Chillicothe with his wife, Patsy. His first book, Knockemstiff, won the 2009 PEN/Robert Bingham Fellowship. His work has appeared in The New York Times, Third Coast, The Journal, Sou’wester, Chiron Review, River Styx, Boulevard, Folio, Granta, NYTBR, Washington Square, and The Berkeley Fiction Review. The Devil All the Time is his first novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12641226759240952259" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
